MEXICO
WATER RESOURCES MANAGEMENT PROJECT
ANNEX D: RESERVOIR OPERATION, DAM SAFETY, AND
AQUIFER MANAGEMENT IMPROVEMENTS
Introduction
1.    This annex discusses the WRMP activities related to improvements in reservoir
operation, dam safety and aquifer management in Mexico. The annex is presented in Tabular
format which provides summary inforrnation on the activities of each of the subcomponents.
For more detail the Project Implementation Plan prepared by CNA should be referenced.
Table D-1: Reservoir Operation
Table D-2: Dam Safety
Table D-3: Aquifer Management
2.    In general terms, these activities presently have the following problems: (a) antiquated
and inefficient models and procedures; (b) insufficient personnel lacking adequate training; and
(c) insufficient and obsolete computer and telecommunications systems.
3.    The WRMP has the basic strategy of gradually moving towards improvements in
reservoir operation, dam safety and aquifer management, learning from initial results and
adjusting the programs along the way. Initial activities and methodologies will be closely
monitored and will be adapted and extended in subsequent actions with as much flexibility
as possible.
4.    The WRMP activities discussed in this annex are focused on improving criteria and
procedures in order to achieve: (a) efficient, modem and effective reservoir operation with
the objective of maximizing the benefits derived from the existing hydraulic infrastructure;
(b) safety of dams in order to assure their continued operation and to avoid damages and
catastrophes; and (c) the effective management of aquifers in order to ensure the
sustainability of their use.



Table D-1
Implementation Plan for the Reservoir Operation Improvement Subcomponent
Background - Dams for water storage were built in Mexico since the colonial times. The water storage sector started to develop strongly in 1926, when the National
Commission for Irrigation was created. Initially, the focus of reservoir development was on storage and some very large dams were constructed. With time, flood control
became more important in the context of a more comprehensive policy. The problem of floods and droughts has been addressed since 1972, with attention paid to normal
(medium and long range) operation of reservoirs and management of emergency situations. A large number of emergencies have been managed since then. The activity met
with many constraints, induding lack of timely and reliable information, lack of adequate data transmission and storage systems, as well as lack of hydrological forecasting
technology.
Two models, OPTIMA and HOWARD, are being used for medium and long range reservoir operation. Simpler methods, such as monthly forecasting equations and probability
curves for reservoir inflows, are also applied. There is a large need for calibration and validation of models and procedures. Development of such procedures meets the
constraints of lack of computer infrastructure, scant data and limited availability of qualified staff.
For flood forecasting, the GASIR rainfall-runoff model is used. Once the flood forecast is obtained, the flood is routed through the reservoir being studied and the downstream
channel. A reservoir operation plan is developed on the basis of the information computed in this way.
The current situation is thus characterized through inefficient antiquated technology, insufficient information to feed the models and an insufficient communication infrastructure  i
to monitor the events and to relay action requirements to the field sites.                                                                                 c;
Adequate reservoir operation and emergency management in Mexico, either in the case of floods or droughts, is hampered because of a dearth of hydrometric, climatological  c
and meteorological data, owing to the insufficient density and quality level of networks and their degradation since roughly 1985. During critical situations, there are not enough
rainfall data available and there are extremely few rainfall intensity reports. Most of the reservoir management information is obtained by voice radio, fax and telephone. The
radio communications network, which has proved particularly valuable in the past, is not only obsolete but increasingly beyond its expected useful life and has depended on
makeshift repairs. Field staff, while generally very willing to carry out its work, is almost totally without technical training to face emergency tasks.
Automatic telemetry systems, which were established in a few critical catchments, collapsed because of lack of maintenance owing to unavailability of funds for maintenance
contracts.
For long range forecasts and annual reservoir management, the existing OPTIMA model used since 1986 has been reasonably successful as far as the recommendations
issued were implemented. Historic information, both on the water supply and the water demand side, has been insufficient or not available in time, resufting in impaired  >
recommendations. Overall, the decision-making process is cast in empirical and subjective approaches. Information of economic aspects of water management is particularly  n
scant. The models used for management of emergency situations have evolved little in the last 20 years and there is a large need for updating to take advantage of new  Gh
technology in this field.
In accordance with the National Water Law, CNA is the national water authority with complete responsibility for water rights administration, the management of the  I
nations water resources and dam safety. Other entities (including CFE) both public and private that operate reservoirs must do so with CNA authorization and
oversight. CFE operates 64 dams with hydroelectric plants. The Director General of CNA presides over a Technical Committee for the Operation of Hydraulic
Works. In addition to CNA authorities and technical personnel, a CFE representative participates on the committee. The committee makes overall decisions relative  I
to the operation of the nation's reservoirs including considerations related to floods, droughts, multiple uses (irrigation, municipal and industrial, hydropower,
navigation, recreation, etc.) and environmental protection. During emergency situations the committee often acts directly in making decisions for reservoir
operations. As the meteorological, hydrological, water quality, groundwater, dam safety, water rights administration and water resources planning and management
services of CNA improve with the WRMP, the availability and reliability of information and the decision making of the committee will also improve, resulting in better
operation of CNA and CFE reservoirs as well as others. As a part of its decentralization actions, CNA is in the process of transferring some of its dams to state  i
governments and to CFE (in the case of CFE, those dams that have hydropower plants). However, CNA will always retain the authority to administer water rights,
manage water resources and for dam safety, and will continue to oversee improvements in reservoir operation through the committee.
Overall Objective - The objectives of this subcomponent are to support the activities of GASIR in improving reservoir operation to maximize the benefits derived from the
operation of existing hydraulic infrastructure by increasing water availability and reducing the adverse consequences of floods and droughts. See CHART D-1 for diagram of
forecasting system.

Background - It is estimated that there are over 4,000 dams in Mexico. Due to size, location, age, inadequate desigr, poor cosructim or lack of mainlara     a signific-nt propetikm
of thse dams may pose a risk to human life, propety, agnculture ard industry downsream
CNA and in pcula CT have considerable expence  in addressing dam safety pblmrns in individual dams of the varous types built in Mexco dunrig several decades ver,
curcntly thee is no omrpreh sive, systematic and effective national dam safely pimgr  in Mexico. Under WRMP the CNA plans to imlement the first phase of a suslainable
national dan safety prgrL  This progran, in conjunction with a second phase (not palt of WRMP) whudi will include rndial d   will result in lorg-term befits by saving lives,
proteing property and cops, and enhacing overall water managenent in the cowmtry.
Responsibility for dam safety in CNA lies with CT of the SGT.
A National Registty of [wms (Registry) was started m 1989 but is currently on hold due to lack of funding. The present Regishy includes 3,200 dams with storage capacity greater than
0.5 million m3; for rnio of thmT, information o their safety conditions is very limited Out of the dams registered, about 18%/ can be classified as large (>1 5m) and approxo,dely 200/o
are older than 30 yars Only about 15% of the registered dams have been inspected; deficiendies of various kinds and degrees 'Aer identified in about 50ffe of them. This high rate of  co
deficienies along with the number of large dams, their age and lack of inspections, are indicative of potentially high safety riskl
Although Owen is an ongoing program to inspect and correct deficiendes at the most critical dams, ther are not enough trained personnel within CNA to keep up with neoded
inspections. FurthemTore there are no standardized dam safety guidelines.
An apprecable number of dams constructed over the past 30 years have been instrumented, Howvever, many of these perfomnance monitoring instmnents have been damaged or
deshtoyed. At present remaining instuunents are only being read at a few dams and data collected from instrument readings is only sporadically analyzed. One important component of a
thorough dam safety prnpamn, is the systematic updating (every five years or so for large dams) of the hydrology of the catchment areas of the dams. The main purpose of this exercise is
to be able to route through the reseroir the updated madximu  estimated flood and verify the sufficiency of the spillway and oher outlet works to handle it, without the dam being
overtopped (which in an embankment dam might lead to breaching). Systematic updating of the probable rnwaimiun flood allows for (if necessary) making corrections to the dam and its
appurtenant structies, thus minimizing the subsequent risk of the dam being overtopped. Accordingly, systematic updating at suitable intervals of the hydrmlogy of selced dams is  in
included nthis isubcomponi
Operation and maintenance manuals do not exist for most dams, and maintenance of existing structures is a problem thxmglout the coumnry due to lack of finding  Likewise risk
ma_gent guidelines related to dams and energency action plans for high risk rsvirs are non exstent
Overall Objectlve - The objectives of this subconronent are to support CT in the imnplenmetation of the first phase of a national damn safety progam, preparation of amgency action j  I
plans and development of risk management guidelines.

Implementation Plan for the Aquifer Management Improvement Subcomponent
Background - Background information on Mexico's overexploitod aquifers problems is summarized in Annex C, Table C-3.
In many instances, in the past 20 to 30 years, the overexploitation or mining of aquifers has made possible the continuing development of agriculture, urban growth, and the expansion of industry.
However, aquifer depletion has produced serious environment degradation problems and severely limited prospects for continued growtLh The general impact has been the increasing pumping
depths and energy costs, saline intrusion in coastal aquifers, land salinization, and severe groundwater pollution by urban and industrial effluents.
The time has come to design a consistent and coherent strategy for optinal use of groundwater resources that addresses among other issues the urgent need for stabilization, restoration and
conservation of aquifers as weU as environment protection.                                                                                                              I
Most initiatives to date to remedy the critical problem of overpumped aquifers - no matter how solid their technical grounding or legal backing - have met with limited success or have failed  e
outright hi a recent shift in approach, CNA has organized an intensive campaign to consult and negotiate with groundwater users. They are being invited to become actively involved in aquifer
management and in monitoring compliance with regulations that are being worked out directly with users.                                                                  f
Such arrangements with users would appear to be the most pronusing approach, from a socioeconomic standpoint in efforts to stabilize and restore overpumped aquifers. Because irrigation
accounts for 70%/o to 80% of the total demand for groundwater, efforts to curtail overpumping should be concentrated in irrigation areas. It would be unrealistic to expect farmers to accept a lower
standard of living or drastically raise yields to compensate for pumping restrictions. Regulations should be phased in, and should include economic inducements to grow high-yield aops that
consume less water and encourage the use of more modem irrigation techniques that save water and cut down on pumping costs.                                             C
0.
Overall Objective -   The objective of the Aquifer Management Improvement subcoinponent is to acquire a prospective vision on citical issues of aquifers management and groundwater  p
protection, and to develop management tools in view to supporting the decision making process.

Table D-3
Implementation Plan for the Aquifer Management Improvement Subcomponent
General Description of WRMP Activities - The managemrent issues, related to technucal as well as socioeconomuc aspects, are especially related to overexploited aquifers and those
undlergouig various degrees of degradation by point-source or non-point-source pollution. Optimrization models need to be developed as management tools in decision making with explhcit
consideration of respective costs and benefits of alternative action progrms. They models would serve as the basis of discussion and consensus building in the firmework ofdecentralized water  X
resources management by the nver basin councils, with fill participation of water users and other stakeholders.
Prospeetive Aquifer Management Studies
a)  Study of the Strategic Role of Groundwater. A spatial analysis of potential supply and demnand of groundwater in the framework ofthe land use orientation defined by the
National Development Plan 1995-2000J ill be carnied out The study will especially take into account, as potential important urban water demand, the programn of 100 Cities  _.
deternined by the Plan                                                                                                                                           O
b)  Study of groundwater withdrawaL In absence of direct measurement, the study will carry out an evaluation of groundwater extraction by different indirect methods which will  r
be cross-checked. These will include the pumping energy consumption, data from the water user registry, satellite imagery for irrigated area evaluation and other statistics data.  0
c)  Study of non point source pollution of groundwater. While there are 2 million hectares of land irrigated by groundwater, there is practically no substantial information and data
on groundwater pollution related to agricultural activities, especially in relation to nitrate concentration. As the same source of groundwater constitutes the principal water supply
source of numerouis muniticipalities and of almost all the ural population, there is an urgent need to assess the agricultural pollution level of aquifers, at the national and regional
scale. Based on its findings, tie study will propose concrete measures to protect and restore the groundwater quality.                                           C
d)  Study of point source pollution control. The analysis will consist of eight case studies in selected areas where point source pollution has been detected. Based on its findings,
the study will propose prevention measures for similar sitmations. 
e) Environmental costs of groundwater overexploitatomn. The analysis will concern ten cases of overexploitation, using the Marginal Oppotunity Costs (MOC) approachL The
econometric model of environmental accounting developed by INEGI will be used for aggregation at the national level.
f)  Water demand management for aquifer stabilization and restorationL Six cases will be selected for analysis and proposal for actions. The proposed actions would analyze all
aspects related to water demand management: technical, socioeconomic, financial, water ight transfer, intra and intersectoral reallocation.
g)  Synthesis of geohydrological units. Groundwater resources management in Mexico is still curTently based on the geohydrological concept of aquifer, the number of which is
estimated at 459. In the framework of a comprehensive approach of vater resources management that recognizes the interactions among various elements of the river basin's
ecosystem, it is necessary to synthesize the geohydrological units into more manageable aquifer systems (according to the systenmc appmach).

MCO
WATER RESOURCES MANAGEMENT PROJECT
ANNEX E: WATER RIGHTS ADMINISTRATION
L Background
I.    Past and current issues in Mexico's water resources management associated with water
policies can be assessed within a concept of efficiency that includes the effectiveness of the legal and
institutional framework, particularly that of water rights administration. See Annex A for a
discussion of the legal and regulatory framework related to water use and development in Mexico.
2.    The National Water Commission (CNA), created in 1989, is the sole federal authority
empowered to manage the national waters. Since 1995, CNA has been relocated from the Ministry
of Agriculture and Hydraulic Resources to the new Ministry of Environment, Natural Resources
and Fisheries. This relocation recognizes water as a basic resource for all economic sectors.
Formerly, it was difficult to separate CNA's role as Authority from its role as promoter of irrigation
development. The confusion caused by the fact of being "judge and party" created some
distortions.
]L Present Situation
3.    In relation to water rights administration, CNA's immediate objective is to regulate water
use and discharge in order to preserve the resource's quantity and quality for a sustainable use and
development. The water use concession and discharge permit with payment of the corresponding
fees is administrated by the Water Administration General Subdirectorate (SGAA) within CNA.
SGAA has the following administrative units:
4.    Services to Users. This unit operates in all states and is in charge of evaluating the
applications, issuing the corresponding concessions and permits, and promoting legal registration of
water rights.
5.    The National Public Water Rights Register is responsible of registering water right titles and
their modification, including all transactions of water rights, as well as certfying water rights and
safeguarding all registered titles and their supporting documents.
6.    The Collection and Control Service is in charge of registering users fiscal statements and the
corresponding payment of fees. It must also provide the necessary information to the Fiscal Affairs
Unit to determine and communicate fiscal charges to users who do not pay. If the omission of
payment persists, the case is turned to the Ministry of Finance and Public Credit to proceed
accordingly. This service must also ensure that the users comply with the conditions and datelines
established in their concessions or permits. Inspection and control visits are carried out to verify the
veracity of water abstraction volumes as well as volumes and quality of wastewater reported by
users.



2                                                  Annex E: Water Rigbts  I inis-Iatica
7.    The Evaluation and Development Unit has the responsibility of supporting the devdpnent
of information systems required for the numerous and complex tasks of the other thee wvics. It
must also integrate, update and distribute formats and procedures, and coordinate training
programs.
A. Registration of Concessions and Permits
8.    When the National Water Law (LAN) was promulgated in December 1992, there wee
three types of water users:
a) regular users, who used national water and its associated land resources and dicged
wastewater within the terms of concessions and permits or of some other typs of
authorization issued by appropriate authorities according to the previous legisation;
b) irregular users, who had some ldnd of authorization but did not comply with all its terms
such as the granted volume of abstrction; and
c) clandestine users, who had no authorization at all.
9.    The Regulations for the LAN, issued in January 1994, have given water users, and
iischargers certain incentives to register their water rights in accordance with the new LAN.
During that year the Comnission gave priority to the registration of large volume users and
polluters. About 80%Yo of the volume of water used is now under legal registration, although only
37,600 users, out of an estimated universe of 300,000, have been regularized. About 5,000
wastewater discharges have also been regularized.
10.   The registration process presently has the following difflicuies:
a) the procedures to entitle new users and to regulaize the others are complex and slow in
processing;
b) irregular users are not willing to regularize their situation due to the fines to be paid;
c) users often do not have some of the documents required by law,
d) in most cases, users apply for larger volumes of water than CNA is willing to grant;
e) low income users, mostly from the irrigation sector, express that they can neithe pay
the fines nor even the charges of issuing the title and permit; and
f) users registered uuilaterally by the Conunission do not pick up thir fitles becase they
are not willing to pay the fines and registration fees.



Annex E: Water Rights Administration                                                3
11.   In September 1995 three presidential decrees were issued to help alleviate some of these
problems. Under these decrees, one-year amnesty periods have been granted during which water
users and dischargers can register without having to pay fines, and in the case of agricultural users,
without have to pay registration fees.
B. Legal Certainty of Water Rights: the Public Water Rights Register
12.   The LAN establishes that the titles, as well as their modifications and transactions, must be
listed in the National Public Water Rights Register (REPDA). The objective is to ensure legal
certainty of water rights and facilitate their modification and transfer, thus fostering the existence of
a market of water rights.
13.   At present, out of the 43,700 regularized users, 37,600 are registered in the official books.
Some users are not willing to register their titles due to the cost of the process and perhaps they are
not aware of the fundamental advantage of being registered in REPDA (only the rights inscribed in
REPDA can be sold or transferred). Some users have expressed their disagreement with the data
that must be registered.
14.   These problems, as well as those concerning the users' regularization process, have inhibited
the expansion of the water rights market, although widespread trading of water rights has already
taken place in and zones.
C. Fees Collection
15.   Important results have been achieved regarding the collection of water use fees, though
several problems prevail. Collections have increased from less than 200 million nuevos pesos in
1989 to almost 1,500 million nuevos pesos in 1994 (wastewater discharge fees amounted to only 50
million nuevos pesos the same year). Potential fee collection would be much higher than what is
actually collected as CNA relies on the users' will to regularize their legal status with the
corresponding fee payment. This strategy requires extensive field inspection and control and
subsequent law enforcement procedure. Other problems making fee collection more difficult
include:
a) most users do not have any measurement device for both abstraction and discharge;
b) municipal water utilities and various major users, such as sugar mills, have not paid their
abstraction and discharge fees arguing their economic difficulties; and
c) the population of potential contributors is not clearly identified.
ILL WRMP
16.   The objectives of the Water Rights Administration component are to consolidate the
enforcement of the LAN, to strengthen the new federalism by securing water rights to all legal



4                                                    Annex E: Water Rights Administraion
water users, to facilitate transaction of water rights, and to improve water fees collection.
Additionally, this component has the objective of providing a basis and a means for the allocation of
water towards more productive uses (i.e. improving the economic efficiency of water use) in a
dynamic setting where water demands are continually changing, while simultaneously protecting
against aquifer depletion, protecting the water rights of third parties, and reducing water pollution.
This is to be done by supporting the effective functioning of water markets that have developed
following the promulgation of the LAN.
17.   Although the government has made good progress in identifying and registering of current
water users and discharges since the 1992 publication of the LAN and its 1994 regulations, there
are still many issues related to the administration of water rights that need to be tackled to ensure
that the water market achieves its desired objectives. Some of these include facilitating the
registration of existing water users, implementing a modified fee schedule for extracting and
discharging water, training CNA staff so that so that they can better protect against aquifer
depletion and possible negative third-party effects, and volumetric pricing.
18. These objectives will be accomplished by carrying out the following sub-components.
A. Services to Users
19.   This sub-component will include the tasks of identifying the population of users and of
improving the service to users for the title registration.
20.   Users identification. This task will be carried out in three stages:
a) general characterization of the user population: using available statistics from various
sources, this activity will aim at identifying the principal categories of users according to
the volumes abstracted and the discharges, their location in relation to municipalities,
states and water basins;
b) cross-checking of the previous information with users listed in REPDA and in the data
base of Fee Collection Service; and
c) users identification with specific targeting (areas of water scarcity, potential payers of
water use and discharge fees, areas with water quality degradation, small users with
water rights to be protected).
21.   Improvement of Services to Users by:
a) the implementation of single counter service;
b) the implementation of computer systems to speed up the administrative procedures of
title registration,
c) the realization of field visits to regularize clandestine users.



Annex E: Water Rights Administration                                              5
22.   All these tasks will be carried out by contracts with consulting firms.
B. REPDA
23.   This sub-component will include the modernization of the operating system of REPDA,
updating the computer system and digitizing of documents, and the data processing capacity for
public communication and publication. These tasks will be carried out by contracts with consulting
firms.
C. Fee Collection and Control
24.   This sub-component will include:
a) The technology development for measurement of water use and wastewater discharge
including: a survey for identification of large users and polluters, a study of norms,
standards and regulations for fair determination of water use fees and discharge fees,
and a pilot scheme for experimentation of new measurement equipment;
b) An accelerated program of inspection and control visits. This program will carry out a
total of 60,800 visits over 6 years.
D. Computing Resources Development
25.   Computer resources to be developed for support of the three previous sub-components will
include:
a) the design and implementation of an integrated information system for all areas of water
rights administration and the networking with other services of CNA; and
b) complete renovation of the obsolete existing computer equipment (20 XT-PCs, 77 AT-
PCs and 14 386-PCs) as well as its expansion.
E. Training
26.   The training effort in water rights administration will aim to develop and expand the
professional skills and capacities of technical staff of CNA central and regional offices and of water
basin councils mainly in two areas:
a) data bases exploitation; and
b) measurement and control of water abstraction and wastewater discharge.

MEXICO
WATER RESOURCES MANAGEMENT PROJECT
ANNEX F: WATER RESOURCES PLANNING AND INFORMATION SYSTEMS
I. Water Resources Planning and River Basin Councils
A. Background
1.    By the early seventies, the Ministry of Water Resources of Mexico had accumulated a great
deal of information and experience in developing the country's water resources. Additionally,
professional groups and institutions related to water planning and management shared the vision of
a integrated national water policy which would support economic growth and social development
while seeking solutions to the increasing problems of water scarcity, depletion of water sources and
water pollution.
2.    In 1975, the first National Water Plan was completed, providing a framework to improve
water resources management consistent with national and regional objectives. In 1976, the National
Water Plan Commission was created to implement the 1975 Plan and update it on a systematic
basis. Also in 1976, the Ministries of Water Resources and Agriculture were transformed into the
Ministry of Agriculture and Water Resources. This change caused some disruption in the water
planning and management efforts. In response to growing institutional problems and to the need of
reinforcing water policies and strategies directed to developing and operating hydraulic
infsucture and improving water use efficiency and pollution controL the National Water
Commission (CNA) was created in 1989 as the sole federal authority dealing with water resources
management.
3.    In 1990, the necessary studies were undertaken to design a new legal instrument. The
resulting National Water Law (Ley de Aguas Nacionales - LAN), enacted in December of 1992,
provides a modem legal framework for water resources management.
4.    An overall assessment of the 1975 National Water Plan was made by the World Bank,
stating: "...comparisons with similar efforts in other countries show that the 1975 National Water
Plan is at least as good, but probably better than what was achieved elsewhere. The only major
effort of this type to date was carried out in 1968-73 in the United States, culmiating in a report
entitled Water Policies for the Future...". The National Water Plan was updated in 1981 and 1994.
During this time the emphasis has shifted from water resources development to conservation and
sustainable use.
B. Present Situation
5.    By the end of the last decade, many water problems had worsened. Scarcity situation were
present in almost two thirds of the territory with the consequent increase in the overexploitation of



2                              Annex F: Water Resources Planning and Information Systems
aquifers; water use efficiency had dropped and water quality had deteriorated. In response to this
situation, and at the beginning of the present decade, new water policies were defined and oriented
in three main directions: development of the infrastructure needed to eliminate existing gaps and
meet increasing demands; achieving greater water use efficiency; and water pollution abatement and
control.
6.    Since 1995, under the new Administration, CNA has been relocated within the new
Ministry of the Environment, Natural Resources and Fisheries both to reinforce the notion of water
as a natural resource to be managed in a sustainable way and to reinforce the country's decision to
abate and control water pollution. The new water policy stresses:
a) integrated water plarning and management both in quantity and quality;
b) decentralization and greater participation of users and society as a whole;
c) introduction of market mechanisms, pricing and other economic incentives to induce
efficient use of water.
7.    The LAN provides a modem legal framework for water resources management. It states
explicitly that sustainable development is its primary objective. Furthermore, the Law is consistent
with CNA's embryonic efforts related to decentralization, water users participation, efficient use of
water, economic instruments and the role of the private sector.
8.    The organized participation of water users and stakeholders is foreseen by the Law as a
mandatory prerequisite for water planning, as well as the formulation of specific management rules
to regulate water use in the framework of the water management unit which is the river basin. The
river basin councils will be the forum where Federal, State and Municipal govemments, the water
users and stakeholders can share the responsibility of planning and managing the basin's water
resources.
9.    The establishment of the Lerma-Chapala River Basin Council has been exemplar in building
consensus for the management of common water resources. Through the Council, water allocation
policies were negotiated in 1992 between the five State Govemments involved with the specific
objective of maintaining adequate water levels and improving the water quality in Lake Chapala, the
countrys largest water body. An investment program was also launched to clean the Lerma River
waters through the construction of 48 treatment plants. As a result the pollutant charge into Lake
Chapala has been reduced by 65%. Participation in the Council's activities has been outstanding.
Water users (irrigation, municipal, industrial, recreation), state governments, federal entities in
addition to CNA-
10.   Drawing from this experience, the Rio Bravo River Basin Council was established in 1994
and has proceeded to implement water allocation policies as well as wastewater treatment programs
to clean the river waters in compliance with Mexico-USA agreements. The establishment of the
Valle de Mexico River Basin Council, in August 1995, has been another important step forward in



Annex F: Water Resources Planning and Information Systems                        3
the decentralization policy as the basin contains the greatest metropolitan area in the world with its
enormous problem of water supply, sanitation and drainage.
C. WRMP
Objectives
11.   The central objective of the subcomponent of Water Resources Planning and River Basin
Council Support is to strengthen and decentralization of water resources management and planning
at the hydrographic region level with the participation of water users and other stakeholders.
12.   The planning task will consist of preparing water resources management strategy which is a
set of medium and long-term action programs to accomplish the country's development objectives
and to implement its water policies. Critical issues in water policies formulation in relation to long-
term development objectives that water resources management strategy must address include:
a) restoration and protection of surface and groundwater quality, over exploited aquifers,
surface water bodies and aquatic and riparian ecosystems to promote the health of all
citizens including the poor, supported by the strategic decentralization of water
resources management with participation of water users and other stakeholders in
decision making;
b) reliance on the private sector, water user associations, water users and other
stakeholders;
c) rural infrastructure development mainly in the water supply and sanitation sector to
reduce urban migration which would require technological innovation to develop
affordable systems in small settlements;
d) increase financial resources with the participation of the private sector in water supply
and sanitation systems;
e) transferring of responsibilities for the operation and maintenance of irrigation systems to
water user associations;
f) improving water use efficiencies in irrigation delivery systems, on-farm and in water
supply systems;
g) improving the allocation of water between sectors; and
h) support for the govenment program (National Development Plan) to develop 100
urban centers to take pressure off the Mexico City Megalopolis.
13 Strategy formulation will incorporate the elements of comprehensive water resources
planning and management, including: (a) institutional and human resources; (b) development of



4                               Annex F: Water Resources Planning and Information Systems
adequate information systems; (c) participation of water users and other stakeholders;
(d) optimization of economic, social and environmental values; and (e) public health considerations.
14.   Strategy formulation by nature will be a dynamnic iterative process. From the foundation of
development objectives and water policies, key issues as those above mentioned will be assessed
and analyzed. The following steps will consist of evaluating options and presenting choices to
policymakers. The final choice of the options by policymakers will become the adopted strategy.
However, options evaluation for strategy formulation may reflect back on development objectives
and water policies which would have to be readjusted.
15.   The River Basin Councils synthesize two fundamental concepts of Mexico's
decentralization water policy:
a) the principal of physical integrity reflected by the basin as a water resources
planning/management unit is explicitly recognized; and
b) the principal of solidarity becomes a reality when users within a basin become aware of
their interrelation with other users and how their actions affect others or conversely how
they are affected by others' actions.
16.   With the gradual establishment of River Basin Councils and the corresponding
decentralization of water resources planning and management, the role of CNA will undergo
considerable change. At the central level CNA will coordinate the development of water policy and
strategy in the context of govemment objectives in the areas of economic development, social well
being, environmental protection, public health, rural and urban planning, decentralization and
intemational treaty obligations. At the regional levels, CNA will support the River Basin Councils
by providing them with technical support in the areas of water resources monitoring, planning,
assessments and management. Regionally, CNA will also function as the Water Authority,
registering water rights and transfers, and monitoring compliance with the LAN.
(a) Regional and National Planning
17.   The formulation of water resources management strategy at the regional and national level
will cover the following main tasks: Diagnostic, Scenarios, Strategy and Plan.
a) Diagnostic: Resources Assessment and Main Issues.
i. Review of socio-economic development objectives and key water related
policies.
ii. Assessment of water resources development and use, including an evaluation of
existing infrastructure. Evaluation of surface and groundwater quality including
an assessment of the point- and non-point-source of pollution, and the nature of
any trends over time.



Annex F: Water Resources Planning and Information Systems                          5
iii. Evaluation of the main issues faced in the water sector, including issues related
to policy, institutional organization, technology, human resources, economic
and financial aspects, planning and the environment.
b) Medium and Long-Term Scenarios
i. Projections of future water demands and supply and presentation of alternative
scenarios for the future water balances based on different assumptions
concerning: population projections and per capita consumption of water,
industrial development, municipal demands, irrigation development and
irrigation efficiency, demand management including overexploited aquifers
stabilization, pricing and marketing issues.
ii. Assessment of altemative future trends with regard to water quality and the
potential mitigating effects of investment in treatment, and regulatory and
incentive measures on aquifer stabilization and restoration.
c) Strategies Formulation
i. Developing options that address the key issues.
ii. Water supply options: development of new sources; reuse; rehabilitation and
modernization of existing infrastructure.
iii. Demand management options: technical measures and economic incentives in
the irrigation sector; water conservation in the urban sector; pricing and
financial penalties; public education and awareness programs.
iv. Water quality options: establishing, monitoring and enforcement of standards;
major areas requiring immediate attention, particularly in relation to
groundwater degradation.
v. Institutional options: harmonizing and making consistent and coherent
regulations; structure and management of water supply and sanitation and
irrigation and drainage organizations; cost recovery and financial autonomy.
vi. Human resources: short and long term training requirements; employment
practices and career structures; expertise and cross-disciplinary skills
development.
vii. Economic options: economic efficiency in water allocation and reallocation.
viii. Environment and public health: protection and reclamation of natural
ecosystems; concept of aquifer reserve as national heritage to be restored and
protected; improving water quality standards; public education.



6                               Annex F: Water Resources Planning and Information Systems
d) Options Evaluation
i. Definition of evaluation criteria.
ii. Evaluation of altemative combinations of options (strategies) to achieve
regional and national objectives, taking into account the following aspects: cost
recovery and water pricing; services decentralization; private sector and
community involvement; risks associated.
e) Regional Plans and National Plan: Orientation Schemes for Water Resources
Management
i. The orientation schemes will synthesize the principal findings of previous
analyses and the set of recommended options, best suited to achieve
development objectives and support key water policies. Presentation of options
in simple language understandable to specialists as well as non-specialists and all
stakeholders.
(b) Establishment of River Basin Councils
18.   The National Water Law, Chapter IV, articles 3 and 13, define river basins as the water
resources management units and the river basin councils as the forum for reaching consensus and
coordination where Federal, State and Municipal governments, the water users and other
stakeholders can participate in the planning and managing of water resources.
19.   CNA is reorganizing its regional offices and has identified 13 hydrographic regions that
cover the entire territory of the country. These regions are presented on the backpiece map and are
denominated as follows:
- Baja California Peninsula
- Upper Northwest
- Lower Northwest
- Central Pacific
- Southern Pacific
- Northern Border
- Northern Central Basins
- Lerma-Santiago
- Northern Gulf
- Central Gulf
- Southern Gulf
- Yucatan Peninsula
- Valley of Mexico



Annex F: Water Resources Planning and Information Systems                        7
20.   As already mentioned, the Councils of the river basins of Lerma-Chapala, Rio Bravo and
Valle de Mexico have been established and will be strengthened. The establishment and
strengthening of the 13 river basin councils will be achieved by the implementation of a series of
activities in each basin. The activities will be divided into four phases, each one with its specific
scope and goal. Each phase will be also be a stage for consensus building prior to the
implementation of the subsequent phase.
a) Phase 0. Constitution of a Technical Group and a Coordination Group in charge
respectively of the technical and institutional aspects of the council establishment. The
Coordination Group will create awareness among water users and stakeholders facing
critical water problems and issues in the basin for their gathering into a solidarity
structure and toward the council establishment. A general communicationrmformation
dissemination program will be initiated to increase public awareness in the basin of
water resources related matters. The Coordination Group will be supported with
technical and socio-economic information, and adequate communication material
prepared by the Technical Group.
b) Phase 1. Diagnostic of the present situation and implementation of an information
system for monitoring. The diagnostic and the information system will cover the
following aspects: assessment of water resources development and use, including an
evaluation of existing infrastructure in the basin; evaluation of surface and groundwater
quality including an assessment of the point- and non-point-sources of pollution, and the
nature of any trends over time; evaluation of the main issues faced in the water sector in
the basin, including issues related to policy, institutional organization, technology,
human resources, economic and financial aspects, planning and the environment.
c) Formulation of Objectives of water quality and water resources allocation.
Dissemination of information on issues and objectives among water users and
stakeholders. Discussion, negotiation, striving for consensus on Objectives.
d) Phase 2. Altemative action plans to achieve the objectives. This phase will correspond
to the preparation of a water resources management strategy of the basin with long-
term planning, fornulation of action programs with their investment costs and recurrent
costs, the implementation agenda, and definition of priorities. Dissemination of
infomiation on the alternative action plans among water users and stakeholders.
Discussion, negotiation, striving for consensus on action plan.
e) Phase 3. Definition of the financing system of the action plan. Formulation of various
alternative options including water fees, tariffs, loans and other financial instruments.
The implementation of a computerized financial information system will permit the
simulation of various hypotheses and determine their viability and sustainability.
Discussion by the river basin council of the financial option.
f) Phase 4. Implementation of a monitoring system of Finance/Environment Performance.
This information system will integrate all the components of the previous systems on



8                              Annex F: Water Resources Planning and Information Systems
water resources, use, planning, action plans and finance into a comprehensive
management system for continuous monitoring of the achievement in environmental
enhancement and the state of finance of the council.
(c) Prospective Studies of Integrated Water Resources Management
21.   The objective of these studies is to analyze the critical issues of sustainable water use and to
provide the necessary information to the regional and national plans. The studies to be carried out
will include:
a) Alternative technology of water supply and sanitation for rural settlements;
b) The role of the private sector in hydraulic infrastructure;
c) The role of water in the agriculture and food sector;
d) Strategy of salinization control in irrigated areas;
e) Watershed management;
f) Optimization model for integrated river basin management;
g) Monitoring and evaluation of on-going irrigation projects;
h) Socio-economic, financial, and regulatory aspects of aquifers stabilization in the 13
hydrographic regions.
IL Telecommunication and Computer Systems and Water
Geographic Information System (SIGA)
A. Background
22.   In 1994 CNA released an "Institutional Program for the Development of Informatics-
Programa Institucional de Desarrollo Informatico" for the period 1995-2000. The Program
provides detailed information concerning the telecommunication, computer resources and
application software currently in use by the management units of CNA The Program proposed the
institutionalization of a committee to coordinate-ordinate the infornatic activities of CNA The
Program also addressed the necessity of training staff and of keeping the skilled telecommunication
and computer professionals at CNA units. The Program recommends more homogeneous
approach for the development of systems and acquisition of hardware and software.
B. Present Situation
23.   In general, the various CNA offices have obsolete and insufficient telecommunications and
computer systems, and inadequate information technology staff. Constant insfitutional changes and



Annex F: Water Resources Planning and Infonnation Systaes                        9
the difficulties to hire and train information technology specialists has lead CNA to deploy
independent information systems at different sub-management units and with different degrees of
successes.
24.   With limited exceptions, data and computer programs are transmitted through the mail, via
FAX or voice telephone communications. Computer storage and processing capacity are woefully
deficient.
25.   Modem Infonmation Technology resources are indispensable for CNA to adequately fulfill
its water resources management obligations. Practical and rapid solutions are required and can only
be achieved with systematic and massive use of telecommunications, computer resources and skilled
staff.
C. WRMP
26.   The objective of the telecommunications and computing resources modemization plan is to
facilitate all CNA technical and administrative offices with basic components to provide an efficient
flow of information between all offices. The objectives include the instalUation of LANs, WANs,
workstations, PC's, and essential software that are adequate and unifomn and provide the basic
elements for common functions such as electronic mail, data base entry and retrieval, analysis of
data, and reports.
27.   Each staff person involved with computing resources (data base input, computation,
analysis, reports, electronic mail, geographic infonnation, simulation modeling, management of
data, projects, and staff; etc.) will have a desktop display adequate to meet their processing
requirements.
28.   The telecommunications and computer resources activities under the WRMP take into
consideration many of the points addressed by the Programia Institucional de Desarrollo Infornmtico
(1995-2000) and provides general design guidance for the implementation of a basic
telecommunication and computer structure for CNA in the period from 1996 to 2001.
(a) Telecommunications
29.   In order to provide an efficient means of information flow from the state offices to the
regional offices and central offices, a telecommunications infrastructure will be designed and
installed. Each state, regional, and central office will have the basics of a local area network. Each
local area network will include wiring from the office's central telecommunications area to each
work site using a technology common throughout CNA. Each office's telecommunications area will
contain local area network hubs and a connection to the CNA's wide area network which will also
be implemented as a part of the WRMP.
30.   The central offices will be inter-connected through the El optical fiber network. CNA will
connect all state, regional, and central offices with an internal wide area network. Each connection
will be a minimum of 64 kilobits per second (kbps), also known as EO, in the first phase, with



10                              Annex F: Water Resources Planning and bnfonmation Sysums
provisions for expansion to 128 kbps when and where required. Two-way satellite-based system
(VSAT) will be used. Each regional office will have a remote telecommunication server and
modems to concentrate data from the stations.
31.   An Internet connection will be established through the Mexican Autonomous University,
which is a main Internet provider in Mexico. A CNA network security policy will be established
from the beginning of the project.
(b) Computing Resourmes
32.   In offices where sub-management disciplines such as SIGA, SMN, GASIR, GAS, GSCA,
etc. are co-located, the computing resources will be shared by the entire office.
33.   Each state, regional, and central office will have two server-class processors, one NT
Wmdows and one Unix, on the local area networkl The NT Wmdows server will facilitate present
and future MS-Wmdows based software tools. Similarly, the Unix server will facilitate present and
future Unix-based software tools such as Oracle and ARC/Info.
34.   The capacity of these servers (number and speed of the CPU's, size of mnemory, disk
capacity, etc.) must meet all minimal software requirements and will be larger in some offices
proportional to the size of the office's needs. Also, the capacity of these servers must be able to
grow over time to meet expanding needs.
35.   Each staff person involved with data base processing analyses, exchange of information,
etc. will have a desktop display adequate to meet their processing requirements. For uniformitty of
access to computing resources within and without each office, each desktop display unit uill have
the ability to display both X-Windows (Unix) and MS-Wmdows.
36.   Except for specialized equipment required to meet pardcular needs, the core computing
resources components will be relatively uniform throughout all the offices. This unifority will
allow procurement to be made in an organized method with great potental for cost savings with
quantity purchases. Because this modernization plan extends over six years, contracts for purchase
and maintenance will be wnitten with the minimum requirement immediately with options to procure
additional resources in the latter years using the same contract.
(c) Data Bases
37.   Because of the unique data base requirements by each disciplie, merging of all CNA data
into a single data base will be difficult, costly and likely impossible. Therefore, each sub-
management discipline will design and deploy a data base management systemn that meets their
specific needs for data storage, computation, retrieval, analysis, and reports. Requiements from
other sub-management disciplines will be requested and met whenever possible, however, the
efficient data base utilization within a discipline will out-weigh extemal demands. A vital element of
each data base design is the ability to import and export data in standard fonnats.



Annex F: Water Resources Planning and Information Systems                        11
38.   To facilitate the transfer of infornation between data bases of differing designs, a data
exchange software tool will be developed. This tool will have the capability of re-formatting data
between standard data base formats.
(d) Water Geographical Information System/SIGA
39.   In this area, the WRMP will support the strengthening and extension of the existing CNA
Water Geographical Information System (SIGA) in view to responding to the rapidly increasing
requirements of various technicaL regional and administrative sectors of CNA. To achieve this
objective, the following tasks will be carried out:
a) Extension of the data base structure for future integration of the information subsystems
to be implemented by WRMP (Groundwater, Water Rights Registry, etc.). This implies
a system redesign;
b) System expansion for remote sensing image processing;
c) General system expansion in georeferenced data processing and system networking; and
d) System implementation in the regional and river basin councils offices.
(e) Staffing Support
40.   Because of its recognized capabilities in the area, a group will be established within GSMN
responsible for the designs, procurement, developments, deployments, maintenance, and
progressive recommendations for core telecommunications and computing resources for all of
CNAN
41.   Each office (state, regional, and central) will have a staff person (or persons proportional to
the size of the office) devoted to general telecommunications and computing resources support.
This person's primary responsibility will be ensuring that all telecommunication and computing
resource components are operational at their site. When failures occur, this person would be
responsible for obtaining repairs, restoring lost or damaged files, etc.
Im Training, Information and Communication
42.   The training effort in integrated water resources planning and management, and information
technology will aim to develop and expand the professional skills and capacities of technical staff of
CNA central and regional offices and of river basin councils. The training program in various
technical, socio-economic, financial and environmental aspects of water resources management and
infonnation technology will comprise: (a) short courses offered by national institutions; (b) short
courses, seminars, and study tours offered by institutions abroad; and (c) national and international
master programs.



12                              Annex F: Water Resources Planning and Information Systems
43.   The communication effort will aim to develop awareness from water users, municipal and
state authorities and other stakeholders about the water issues in their hydrographical basins and the
necessary shared common effort and solidarity to achieve the common objective sustainable water
resources development. In addition to the information meetings, seminars and workshops which
will be organized at each stage of the river basin council establishment, study tours will be organized
for the representatives of councils members for experience exchange with river basin councils
abroad.

G-1   Projects Involving the Provision of Meteorological/Hydrological Service and
