MEXICO
WATER RESOURCES MANAGEMENT PROJECT
ANNEX G: ECONOMIC ASPECTS, PROJECT BENEFITS,
AND ENVIRONMENTAL ASSESSMENT'
L Project Objectives and Strategy
A. Strategic Responses to Water Management Challenges
1.    In response to growing water scarcity and deteriorating water quality in Mexico, the CNA
has intensified its focus on three distinct, but interrelated, activities: planning; the establishment of
incentive-based institutions; and infrastructure reform. In terms of effective water resources
planning, the CNA recognizes the importance of water user and other stakeholder participation in
the preparation and implementation of any water plan. The CNA's focus is therefore on the
promotion of basin water plans, the preparation and implementation of which take place at the
local level through River Basin Councils that consist of representatives of water users and other
interested private and non-private organizations in the basin.
2.    While the creation of local, River Basin Councils can be viewed as an activity related to
the creation of incentive-based institutions, the CNA is in the process of creating a number of
other institutions of this character, provisions for which are an important part of recent federal
legislation. Examples are as follows.
a) The CNA is presently completing a program that transfers responsibilities for
the operation and maintenance of irrigation facilities to water user associations
in irrigation districts.
b) The CNA is in the process of completing a National Registry of water users
which establishespfngible usufructuary water rights that can be traded in water
markets.
c) The CNA has established a system of water prices, paid by municipal and
industrial water users, in an effort to provide incentives for water conservation.
d) As provided by the Federal Rights Law, the CNA is in the process of
completing a registry of industrial firms that discharge wastes into water bodies
(rivers, lakes, and ponds) and of establishing industry-specific quality standards
for discharged wastes. All unregistered dischargers are subject to a fine.
3.    Finally, looking to infrastructure reform as a key element of the CNA's strategic response
to the water/environmental challenges that it faces, the CNA proposes to expand, upgrade and
modernize the information-based infrastructure that supports the nation's water uses and water
I     This document presents a sununary of the analyses. A more detailed stand-alone document is in
the process of being prepared.



2                    Annex G: Economic Aspects, Project Benefits, and Environmental Assessment
quality and, through an intensive training program, to upgrade the human capital required for the
effective use of that infrastructure.
B. Information as the key to the CNA strategy: Objectives of the WRMP
4.    It is surely obvious that reliable data concerning water quantity and quality, "reliable" in
the sense of being comprehensive in term of content, time, and space, are fundamental requisites
for success in the implementation of the CNA's strategy described above. Basic to this strategy is
a process of decision-making and policy formulation regarding the operation and management of
water resources systems, the enforcement of water use and quality regulations, and investments;
the quality of such decisions, and the effectiveness of such policies, will depend critically on the
quality of the data and information that are available to the CNA's decision makers.
5.    The following examples may serve to further obviate the critical importance of reliable
information for these functions.
a) Hydrographic regional planning as contemplated by the CNA's strategy for
River Basin councils will necessarily involve trade-offs and sacrifices if water
uses are to be brought into congruence with existing water supplies and
substantive improvements in water quality are to be realized. The substance of
considerations by stakeholders of plans that entail the sharing of sacrifices is
directly related to stakeholders' confidence in the reliability of data used to
compare alternative plans.
b) Improved efficiency in the management and operation of surface water
supplies, as well as operationally efficient and effective responses to flood and
drought conditions, requires reliable and timely hydrometric and
meteorological data. The provision of these data require access to modern,
state of the art, meteorological technologies and facilities, as well as modem
telemetric and radio communications systems.
c) If the CNA, in conjunction with the River Basin Councils, is to effectively
promulgate policies to reduce groundwater overdrafts it must have detailed
data and assessments that characterize affected aquifers. Such data and
assessments derive only from modern, comprehensive monitoring and
information systems and modem computerized modeling techniques.
d) The promulgation and enforcement of water quality standards is simply
unworkable without reliable water quality data. The availability of such data
requires the existence of state of the art water quality laboratories, the
establishment of national water quality monitoring and information systems,
and water quality assessments.
e) The CNA's strategy involving reliance on some forms of water markets is predicated
on, first, reliable information concerning water availability, and second, on the
existence of well-defined water rights. The establishment of such rights is dependent



Annex G: Economic Aspects, Project Benefits and Enviromnental Assessment           3
on the completion of the National Water Rights Registry.
f) Dam safety is an important and integral part of the CNA's plans to improve the
efficiency and effectiveness of water resources management at reservoir
facilities. Standards for dam safety, required investment programs for
upgrading and/or redesigning reservoir configurations, and effective emergency
preparedness programs require information and data that derive from:
extensive dam inspection; hydrological evaluation of dams; design studies for
corrective actions; instrumentation and data analyses; and real-time monitoring
systems.
6.    The overall objective of the WRMP is therefore seen as that of providing the facilities,
equipment and training required to generate the fundamental information needs of the CNA's
strategy for responding to water quality and quantity management challenges in the contemporary
Mexican environment.
II. Benefits Associated With The WRMP: Approach
7.    The standard approach to assessing the feasibility of a proposed project is referred to as
cost-benefit analysis. Cost-benefit analysis typically involves comparisons of the net value of a
project-related increase in the production of goods and services with the project's cost. The
nature of the WRMP does not lend itself well to this standard approach. The objective of the
WRMP is neither a direct increase in the production of goods and services nor a direct increase in
social welfare however measured. Its objective is to "produce" data and information.
Information clearly has value. As noted by Mosley, "Information may be said to be the 'antidote'
to uncertainty, or the 'raw material from which decisions are made." Thus, it is the use of the
WRMIP's data and information -- the use of this "raw material" -- that can be expected to directly
impact social welfare through a combination of such things as increasing agricultural production
(via increased efficiency of water use), expanding opportunities for industrial growth (via, as one
example, reallocating water for municipal and industrial uses), ameliorating or eliminating the
mining of exhaustible natural resources thereby promoting sustainable growth, reducing risks and
costs associated with dam safety, floods, and drought, improving environmental quality, and more
generally, improving decision-making processes.
8.    Benefits or values associated with the WRMP are therefore seen as deriving from
enhancements in the way that information is organized in the sense of data collection and data use
in decision making processes. The value of information can be very difficult to measure. It is
then typically the case that benefits for projects like the WRMP must be estimated by means that
rely upon measures that are inferential, anecdotal, and indirect in nature. The approach to be used
here for an assessment of the potential feasibility of the WRMP makes use of all three of these
measures.
mII. Precedents For Feasible WRMP-like Projects
9.    Table G-1 provides examples of results from studies in a large number of countries that
attempt to estimate benefit-cost measures for the relevant country's expenditures on new,
upgraded technologies that have been put in place for improving the quality of hydrologic and



4                   Annex G: Econornic Aspects, Project Benefits, and Environmental Assessment
meteorological data. These results provide overwhelming evidence of the benefits that accrue to
society from investments that have the effect of enhancing the quality of meteorological and
hydrological data.
10.   The source of benefits measured in the bulk of the studies cited in Table G-1 derive from
such things as benefits (to agriculture, public health and safety, transportation, industrial and
public sectors) from improved weather forecasts, reservoir management, and water resources
planning. There are still other studies that provide compelling indicators of the feasibility of
investment project that enhance the quality of hydrological data for other dimensions of end-uses
that are akin to those relevant for the WRMP. As but a few examples, benefit-cost ratios for
projects that upgrade facilities for collecting and disseminating streamfiow data are shown to
range from 2.8:1 to as high as 21.2:1. Benefit-cost measures for collecting and archiving data
used for the design, sizing, and management of water impoundment facilities have been reported
to range from 1.7:1 to 2:1.
11.   A sub-component of the WRMIP's meteorological component has as its purpose the
commercialization of hydrological and meteorological information. A number of countries have
realized remarkable success in forming public-private partnerships along the lines contemplated by
the WRMP. As but one example, a collaborative program between the Japanese Meteorological
Agency and 20 private meteorological companies serves an estimated market for weather
information that amounts to around US $200 million/year.
IV. Anecdotal Indications Of the WRMP's Feasibility
12.  In this section attention is turned to a brief examination of two sets of selected experiences
in Mexico that relate to the quantity and quality of hydrological and meteorological data. One set
concerns the magnitude of damages born by the Mexican population arising from
hydrometeorological phenomena, damages some part of which would surely be affected by the
WRMP. The second set concerns Mexico's experiences with what might be regarded as a pilot
project for the formation of River Basin Councils as a central part of basin water planning. The
purpose of these examinations is to provide a basis, however limited and perhaps speculative, for
drawing inferences as to values which might reasonably be expected to accrue to Mexican society
from the WRMNP.
A. The Potential Value of Enhanced Meteorological and Hydrological
Data From Reduced Flood and Drought Damages
13.   Mexico's geography and topography, with its extended coastlines and concentrations of
people and economic activity in relatively close proximity to the Atlantic and Pacific coasts, make
it extremely vulnerable to effects of hurricanes and northward moving cold fronts. Annual
damages from all hydrometeorological phenomena (rain storms, floods, droughts, freezes, hail and
wind storms) in Mexico during the period 1987-1994 were: a partial or total loss of production
from some 1.4 million hectares; a loss of some 38,000 head of livestock; and costs associated with
human suffering resulting from the destruction of more than 27,000 homes, more than 133,000
required to evacuate their homes, a loss of life for 181 people, and serious injuries suffered by
more than 100 people.



Annex G: Economic Aspects, Project Benefits and Environmental Assessment           5
14.   The largest part of these damages from hydrometeorological phenomena arise from floods
and droughts. In terms of monetary losses, primarily in the agricultural and forestry sectors, the
greatest losses are caused by drought which average some 1 billion NP (1996 pesos) per year.
Average annual monetary losses from floods are some 302 million NP. Among knowledgeable
CNA professionals there is little question that the magnitude of these losses would be substantially
reduced with more reliable hydrometeorological forecasts, and more reliable and more timely data
concerning streamflow.
15.   Among other things, these examples make clear the urgent need for the acceleration of
efforts to modernize Mexico's hydrometeorological forecasting capabilities.
B. Basin Planning With Community Involvement:
The Lerma-Chapala Experience
16.   As was mentioned above in Section 1.B, an important element in the CNA's strategy for
improving water management in Mexico is the creation of River Basin Councils made up of
representatives of stakeholders in the basin that will play a major role in the preparation and
implementation of basin-wide water management plans. WRMP's financial resources would be
allocated to these and related planning and management activities which involve the collection,
use and analysis of hydrometeorological data and information. One can then reasonably inquire:
on what basis might one expect that such an expenditure is feasible in a benefit-cost sense?
17.   Such a basis is provided by Mexico's experience with what might be regarded as a pilot
project for the creation of River Basin Councils: the existing Lerma-Chapala River Basin Council.
Major water resources-related concerns in this Basin have been: unsustainable withdrawals
(mining) of water from the Basin's groundwater resources; over use of surface water resources;
and dramatic declines in water quality in Lake Chapala -- a unique environmental and recreational
asset to the region and to Mexico. The decline in Lake Chapala's water quality was attributable to
a combination of two sets of conditions that had evolved over previous decades: a decline in
freshwater infusions, particularly from the Lerma River, as a result of continuously increasing
upstream diversions of water for irrigation; and the contamination of river waters from
unregulated and untreated waste discharges by municipalities and industries.
18.   It is instructive to review a few of the major accomplishments -- successes -- and major
failures of this pilot project. The following are the major accomplishments of the Council over
the relatively brief period 1991-1994. In terms of major accomplishments: 48 new treatment
plants, treating more than 1.5 billion m3 of waste water per year, have been constructed in the
Basin with more than 30% of the costs being paid by private or semi-private concessions; an
agreement was established among stakeholders to reduce surface water diversions in the Basin
and to make a specific allocation of water to Lake Chapala.
19.   These are remarkable accomplishments by any standards, and more so (given Mexico's
past experiences) given the fact that the bulk of these actions were initiated and implemented
under the auspices of a private-government partnership: the River Basin Council. A primary
lesson learned from this experience is the potential effectiveness of basin water plans when local
groups, including Governors of affected States, are directly involved in the planning and
implementation process.



6                    Annex G: Economic Aspects, Project Benefits, and Environmental Assessment
20.   Lessons learned from the River Basin Council's failures are equally instructive for the
purposes of the assessment of the WRMP. The following are a few examples of such failures and
lessons. First, the CNA's Regional Office in the Basin has been notably unsuccessful in enforcing
water quality regulations, a lack of success is attributed to one major cause: the lack of reliable
data. Second, it is generally recognized that substantial increases in the availability of water could
result from still greater increases in the efficiency of water use in the irrigation sector. Existing
inefficiencies in that sector derive, in large part, from the antiquated infrastructure which is used
to distribute water for irrigation use. Thus, the rehabilitation and modernization of the Basin's
irrigation infrastructure could accomplish a great deal in terms of making additional water
supplies available for high valued uses. Finally, effects of floods which are recurrent events in the
Basin could be substantially reduced by expanding the number of streamflow measurement
stations in the Basin (there are now only 27), and by improving the quality of facilities and
equipment used for the provision of hydrological and meteorological data and for modeling and
analyses of the data.
V. An Indirect/implied Indicator of WRMP's Feasibility
21.   An indirect indicator of the WRMP's feasibility can be developed in the following manner.
There are many activities and events in Mexican society that will be affected by the project.
Unfortunately, only a small part of these are amenable to measurement in terms of monetary
values that are associated with them. The activities and events for which monetary values can be
estinated are the following. Values given below are average annual values measured in millions
of 1996 NP.
Agricultural damages from floods:                          302
Agricultural damages from drought:                        1,020
Social costs of lives lost in floods:                     1,080
Hydroelectric power production:                           6,800
Social costs of degraded water quality:                  13,611
Potential marketing of water rights; user
payments for water use and discharge:                   12,796
Costs of groundwater mining:                              2,725
Value of irrigated agricultural production:              _,
129,047
22.   These activities, annual values for which total 129,047 million NP, will be affected by the
project. However, it is not known nor can it be known the magnitude by which these values will
be affected. Annualized project costs are 230 million NP. It can then be asked: by what amount
would the values given above need to be affected for the project to be feasible (i.e., have a ratio
of benefits to costs that equals or exceeds unity)?
23.   The measure relevant for responding to this question is determined by dividing 230 million
NP by 129,047 million NP, which yields .00 18. This means that if the project were to affect the
values given above by only about two-tenths of one percent, the project would indeed be
economically feasible. The question then becomes: is it reasonable to expect such effects?



Annex G: Economic Aspects, Project Benefits and Environmental Assessment            7
24.   A 0. 18% effect on virtually all of the values given above is surely more than plausible. For
example, in terms of flood damages only, such an effect from improved meteorological forecasts
would derive from a reduction in the annual loss of life from 181 to 150 -- a saving of 31 lives
which is valued at 230 million NP. Would it be "reasonable" to expect effects of this kind? For
perspective regarding this question, consider the following. In the Netherlands the estimated
number of lives saved each year from reduced traffic accidents only, a reduction that is
attributable to improved meteorological information, is estimated to be between 25 and 63
lives/year; a comparable estimate of some 200 lives saved per year is reported for the United
Kingdom.
25.   As another example, referring only to potential payments of user fees for water use and
water discharge, the importance of these payments derives from, among other things, the
incentives that would be placed on polluting entities to rationalize the management of discharges
of residual waters into the nation's streams via optimal investments in pollution-abatement
technologies -- an optimization process for which a basic prerequisite is reliable information. In
this regard, the requirement, as a condition for a feasible project, that these payments be affected
by less than two-tenths of one percent is virtually trivialized by the CNA's demonstrated success
with a pilot project wherein the ratio of collections to expenditures was on the order of 12:1 --
i.e., a demonstrated "benefit-cost" ratio of 1 181 :1.
26.   Referring finally to a "required" (for project feasibility) two-tenths of one percent
reduction in the social costs associated with groundwater mining, on its face it is surely reasonable
to accept such a modest measure of success for the WRMiP: it would require reductions in
groundwater mining in an amount equal only to the amount of water required to irrigate some 6
to 7 hectares. The gross underestimate of values that are potentially affected by the project,
resulting from both omitted values and understated values described above, and the small -- two-
tenths of one percent -- impact on such values required for project feasibility should suffice to
persuade even the most skeptical as to the project's feasibility.
VI. The Economic Feasibility of the WRMP: Conclusions
27.   In this Annex, it has been shown that expost analyses of investment programs designed to
improve the reliability of hydrological and meteorological information in many counties
throughout the world suggest that such programs, like the WRMP, yield benefits that greatly
exceed costs. Anecdotal evidence has been provided of the potential benefits of the WRMP by
examining specific instances in which more timely access to more reliable hydrological and
meteorological information would have resulted in obvious benefits to Mexican society. And it
has been demonstrated, using data that significantly understate potential values relevant for
Mexico, that one's conclusion that the WRMP is indeed feasible requires only the most modest
expectations for its success in affecting values of social significance in Mexico.
28.   Of course, the reader will draw his or her own conclusions as to the plausibility of
expecting the WRMP to have the "modest" impacts on relevant social values estimated in this
section. This discussions of WRMP's feasibility closes with the following observations which
might be of interest to the reader in this regard. Referring to the "expectation" of a project's
impacts on social values -- 0. 18% in the case of WRMP, i.e., a "required" rate of return of 0.18%
-- one might well inquire: what have been such expectations for a required rate of return in other



8                   Annex G: Economic Aspects, Project Benefits, and Environmental Assessment
countries? Two examples are offered. Referring to criteria for hydrological and meteorological
programs in the U.K., the common goal of such programs is to achieve a minimum threshold rate
of return of 6%. Finally, Indian engineers reportedly looked to a 10% savings in overdesign costs
as justifying a WRMP-like project.
VLL Environmental Assessment of the WRMP - Frame of Reference
29.   As is the case with "traditional" cost-benefit analysis, discussed above, the WRMP does
not lend itself well to the "traditional" environmental impact statement. The primary purposes of
the WRMP do not directly affect processes that produce goods and services, that directly employ
human resources, that make use of natural and environmental resources, or that directly affect air
and or water quality. Moreover, in the case of the WRMP there is little in the way of any one-to-
one relationship between the several project components and any particular set of environmental
and social effects. In any substantive terms, such components share a common and inextricably
related goal: improving the quantity, quality, and timeliness of hydrological and meteorological
information available in Mexico, and enhance the quality of water resources planning in the
country.
30.   Given these unique characteristics of the WRMP vis a vis conventional water reclamation
projects, the approach to assessing social and environmental aspects of the project must be
correspondingly unique. A qualitative description of many of these social and environmental
aspects has been given above, and little can be gained by repeating them here. The dramatic
changes in the CNA's approach to basin wide planning, via reliance on river basin councils which
is a critical activity supported by the WRMIP, has been described, as have been the activities
contemplated by the WRMP that relate to dam safety programs and the management of reservoir
systems.
31.   Social and environmental aspects of the WRMFP that were not fully explored above
concern water quality in Mexico, and the potential effects on this important Mexican resource that
may derive from the WRMP. The intended contribution of this section is therefore to explore
these aspects.
A. Social and Environmental Objectives of the Project
32.   Chapter XIV of the Federal Rights Law contains two provisions that are of critical
importance for the CNA's mandates for improving water quality: the CNA is given a mandate to
develop water quality standards (Official Mexican Guidelines and Specific Discharge Provisions)
which must be met by entities that discharge waste waters in the public domain; and municipal and
industrial entities that discharge waste waters in the public domain ("water bodies," surface
waters, surface impoundments, and groundwater) are required to obtain permits (to be registered,
or "administratively controlled") for such discharges and to pay a fine when chargers fail to obtain
proper authorization and/or that fail to comply with water quality standards.
33.   For the CNA to effectively respond to the mandates of current environmental legislation,
given that it is effectively starting at ground zero, it faces enormous challenges in terms of the
generation of data requisite for the promulgation of water quality standards, which by law must be
industry specific, and for the myriad activities required for the enforcement of such standards:



Annex G: Economic Aspects, Project Benefits and Environmental Assessment         9
identifying dischargers and associated characteristics of discharged waters; monitoring ground and
surface water quality; and enforcement/inspection procedures.
34.   The objectives of the CNA, and with the WRMP's support of the CNA substantial
objectives of the WRMP, are to meet these challenges. The CNA has recently completed a partial
inventory of water use and waste discharges that provides a point of departure for efforts required
to respond to its mandates. To date, the CNA's attention has focused on but four measures of
water quality: biological oxygen demand (BOD), chemical oxygen demand (COD), total
suspended solids (TSS), and grease and oils (GyO); thus, the CNA has yet to consider measures
and standards for metals and other toxic contaminants.
B. The Present State of Water Quality in Mexico.
35.   A large part of Mexico's population lives in communities whose wastewater is not treated
before being discharged into rivers and streams. Even in large urban areas with populations in
excess of 80,000 only about one-fourth of the population's wastewater is treated.
36.   More specifically in 1994 an estimated 174 m3/s of wastewater were collected through
municipal sewage systems (4,945 metric tons of BOD/day), and the main industries generated
about 65 m3/s of wastewater (4,380 metric tons of BOD/day). An estimated 17 m3/s (410 metric
tons of BOD/day) of municipal wastewaters are treated adequately of a total of 43 m3/s of
existing capacity, and 5.3 m3/s (329 metric tons of BOD/day) of industrial wastewaters are
treated. The metropolitan area of Mexico City alone discharges an estimated 42 m3/s wastewater,
only 17% of which is treated.
37.   Relatively little appears to be known about water contamination from agricultural
activities in Mexico.
C. CNA Plans for Improving Water Quality
38.   According to the National water Plan 1995-2000, by the year 2000 there will be a 13.5%
increase in the number of people (an increase of about 10 million people) served by a water supply
system, and a 23% increase in the number of people connected to wastewater systems. Some 82
m3/s of municipal wastewaters are expected to receive treatment by the year 2000.
39.   By Mexican law businesses/industries that discharge waste waters into streams are
required to register such discharge with the CNA. Registered firms then pay a fee for discharges
based on volume discharged, and must pay fines if the quality of their discharges exceed standards
established by the CNA. The CNA has encountered a number of problems in the effective
enforcement of these laws, however. This lack of success derives from firms' challenges to CNA-
imposed sanctions in the courts and the CNA's lack of access to data required to prove up its
claims -- it has great difficulty in proving that contamination in any one stretch of the river is
caused by a particular industry's waste discharges. In these regards, it is simply the case that the
CNA has too few monitor facilities in the nation's rivers and streams. Moreover, the CNA has
the legal power to conduct on-site tests of a company's discharges, but is required to give the
company a 24 hour notice; not surprisingly, on the day of the CNA's visit to a company, it is not



10                    Annex G: Economic Aspects, Project Benefits, and Environmental Assessment
rare to find the company operating at less than normal capacity and/or not running particular
processes.
40.   A major component of the WRMP is the assistance of the CNA's efforts to complete and
make operational the National Registry of Water Discharges and to expand the development of
industry-specific water quality standards for waste discharge. Included in this program is an
intensive program of inspection and measurement visits of 3,300 industrial firms over the period
1996-2000 for the purpose of "administratively controlling" industrial discharges of residual
waters. This program expands a pilot project wherein 99 industrial firns were intensively audited
by the CNA in terms of the quantity and quality of their discharges of waste waters into public
water bodies. At a cost of 1.6 million NP, the project resulted in the collection of 18.9 million
NP in unpaid (over some 4-+ years) discharge fees (an amount that would have been greater had
the CNA had access to the kinds of data that would have allowed their successful response to
industry challenges of their findings). The implicit benefit/retum-to-cost ratio suggested by
results from this pilot project -- 11.8-to-I -- has obvious implications for environmental returns
associated with this data acquisition component of the WRMP.
D. Concluding Remarks
41.   The potential contributions to Mexico's efforts to improve water quality is perhaps
obvious from the discussions of the magnitude of water pollution in the nation given above.
Investment planning for water treatment facilities clearly benefits from the quality and timeliness
of data concerning surface and groundwater quantity and quality, the enhancement of such data
systems and improved modeling and assessments is an important part of the WRMP. Annual
social costs associated with water contamination are estimated to amount to some 5,725 million
1996 NP, a level of costs which must surely be at the lower bound of "real" costs when one
considers that this estimate excludes morbidity and mortality costs associated with contaminated
waters in Mexico.
42.   From the above discussions, it is clear that of primary importance for considerations
related to the improvement of water quality in Mexico are direct discharges of waste waters into
the public domain by industries -- discharges only 7% of which are treated. Full compliance by
industries with Mexico's current laws would result in annual payments of fees and fines on the
order of 13 billion NP -- a level of payment that is not forthcoming at the present time. The
importance of such collections derives from the incentives that would be placed on polluting
entities to rationalze the management of discharges of residual waters into the nation's streams
via optimal investments in pollution-abatement technologies. If the CNA is to make substantive
progress in bringing about industry compliance with water quality standards it must have the data
and information required to, first, establish such standards, and second, to enforce them. The
WRMP provides the support required by the CNA to initiate programs requisite for such
progress.
43.   Perhaps conspicuous by its absence from discussions given above is a dimension of
Mexico's environmental quality that is of particular importance for considerations related to
sustainable growth and development: the quality of the nation's stock of groundwater. The
nation's groundwater stocks are being depleted from enormous annual overdrafts, with an
estimated annual cost of more than 2 billion NP, raising serious sustainability questions, and are



Annex G: Economic Aspects, Project Benefits and Environental Assessment 11
being contaminated by unregulated discharge of waste waters. The problem, however, is that
little is known in terms of aquifer characteristics that are central to any effort to ameliorate and/or
eliminate overdrafts and contamination. An important component of the WRMP is designed to
support Mexico's efforts to develop the hydrogeological monitoring systems and modeling and
assessments required to provide the information relevant for the management of this important
natural and environmental resource.
44.   In closing, mention was made above of a critically important element in the CNA's
strategy for improving water management in Mexico: the WRMP-supported creation of River
Basin Councils, made up of representatives of stakeholders in the basin, that will play a major role
in the preparation and implementation of basin-wide water management plans which, in turn, will
be the primary vehicle by which environmental quality is affected in Mexico's water basins. If
basin planning is to be effective in promoting environmental/water quality, it will be particularly
important for the CNA to assure that the Councils have available to them individuals with the
expertise requisite for the design and implementation of environmental plans. Technical analysis
support to the Councils will be provided by the CNA regional offices. These offices have
developed regional Project Implementation Plans for the WRMP which include taking into
account environmental factors in all aspects of river basin planning and management. Each
regional office will designate or hire as well as train an environmental specialist who will be
specifically responsible for ensuring environmental adequacy in all aspects of water resources
management.

MEXICO
WATER RESOURCES MANAGEMENT PROJECT
ANNEX H: PROJECT SUPERVISION, MONITORING, AND EVALUATION
I. Organization
1.    Overall project supervision, monitoring and evaluation will be carried out by SGP
assisted by project coordination staff. Additionally, one technical person will be located in
each of the regional offices and in the various technical units (i.e. SGT, GASIR, GSMN, GAS,
GSCA, CT, SGAA and SGP). The technical unit and the regional office coordination staff will
be responsible for monitoring and evaluating the implementation of the portion of the project
under their responsibility. The coordination staff located in SGP will be responsible for overall
project coordination and supervision.
2.    An Executive Committee made up of the CNA Subdirector Generals of SGP, SGT,
SGAA and SGA. The former will act as President of the Committee. The project Coordinator
in SGP will act as Technical Secretary to the Executive Committee. The Executive Committee
will be responsible for: (a) directing the implementation of the WRMP in accordance with the
directives of the Director General of CNA; (b) reviewing and approving the annual budget and
work and procurement plans (Planes Operativos Anuales - POAs) for the national and regional
levels of CNA; (c) reviewing and approving changes in the POAs recommended by the
different technical area offices the regional offices and/or the SGP coordination staff; and
(d) reviewing the progress reports prepared by the project coordination staff and adopting or
recommending corrective actions as required.
3.    The overall project coordination staff will be under the direction of the Subdirector
General of SGP and will be responsible for coordinating all aspects of project implementation.
Specifically the coordination staff will: (a) facilitate and promote the institutional interaction
between the various technical and regional offices and entities outside CNA involved with the
project; (b) keep project evaluations and implementation schedules and programs updated,
recommending and making modifications as required; (c) support the programming, budgeting
and implementation aspects of the project, maintaining an integral/comprehensive view; and
(d) prepare the POAs coordinating the input from the various technical areas and from the
regional offices. Project coordination will be support by a consulting firmn that will carry out
detailed monitoring, evaluating and reporting in accordance with the PMS discussed below.
The SGP project coordination staff will have one coordinator, two assistants and necessary
support staff.
II. Indicators related to Project Objectives
4.    Table H-3 presents a listing of indicators directly related to project objectives. These
indicators are linked to specific objectives and project activities that are designed to resolve
specific deficiencies related to the objectives. See Tables H-1 and H-2. These indicators, their



2                                 Annex H: Project Supervision, Monitoring and Evaluation
baseline values at specific stages of project implementation and their target values were agreed
upon during negotiations.
III. Hierarchical-Tree Indicators
5.    Table H-4 presents a summary of the most important input, output, outcome and
impact indicators. The nature of these indicators and their baseline values at specific stages of
project implementation and their target values were agreed upon during negotiations.
IV. Programming, Budgeting, Monitoring and Evaluation
System and Computerized Support System
6.    A programming, budgeting, monitoring and evaluation system supported by a
computerized operational system (Project Management system -PMS) would be developed and
initialized to support project supervision and evaluation prior to the initiation of the project. It
will be an information and operation system based on the development and use of a data base.
The PMS will contain and make available information that will permit the continuous
monitoring of each activity in each phase of project implementation. The information will be
included in the PMS in a manner that will facilitate the programming, budgeting, monitoring
and evaluation processes. The PMS will be designed in order to generate tables and reports
that can be used to evaluate the physical and financial implementation processes of the project.
7.    The PMS will be structured with five subsystems: Planning, Programming, Execution,
Monitoring and Evaluation.
8.    Planning . The planning phase includes the activities related to project preparation
where objectives, strategies, and general policies are developed, and the general project
physical and financial goals and targets are established. During the implementation phase the
planning process will include the periodic updating of these project baseline concepts.
9.    Programming. The programming phase is the development of POAs. In accordance
with available fiscal and World Bank loan funds, detailed annual plans are developed for
project implementation that include both physical and financial goals and targets. The POAs
will be developed detailing the activities, responsible parties, financial resources requirements
to be carried out in each project subcomponent. The POAs are reviewed by the Executive
Committee and by the World Bank.
10.   Execution. The execution phase includes the carrying out of all the activities defined
in the POAs. It includes: (a) development of terms of references, technical specifications and
bidding documents; (b) contract bidding, selection, administration and supervision processes;
(c) direct undertaking of activities to be carried out directly by CNA; and (d) execution of
training and technical assistance programs This phase also includes the flow of finarinia!
resources in order to execute the POA activities.



Annex: H: Project Supervision, Monitoring and Evaluation                       3
11.   Monitoring. The monitoring phase includes a verification of the satisfactory
completion of the different activities, or the identification of problems and/or delays.
12.   Evaluation. The evaluation process will include a determination of why or why not
goals and targets are being reached, and identifying the reasons and suggesting actions to the
respective directors. The evaluations will be used as feedback for the planning and
programming processes. The evaluation process will also include recommendations for
changes to the current and subsequent POAs, as necessary.
13.   Design of System. The PMS will be designed to include: (a) the flows of information,
feedback and decision making, and the interrelationships between the distinctive functions,
technical areas, and regional offices; (b) the formats and procedures for capturing data; (c) the
requirements and procedures for report generation; and (d) the development of procedural
manuals for PMS operation.
V. Supervision and Monitoring Process
14.   Based on the information in the POAs about Physical and financial goals, targets and
schedules, the project coordination staff will monitor and coordinate the implementation of the
project. The different dependencies of CNA (central and regional) will prepare quarterly
reports that will be provided to the SGP coordination staff for this process. These reports will
contain all of the necessary physical, financial and administrative detail for the quarter under
consideration.
15.   The SGP project coordination staff will prepare semi-annual reports which will be
submitted to the World Bank within 60 days following the end of each semester. These
reports will contain the results of the monitoring and evaluation from the previous six-month
period and proposals for modifying the current planning and programming processes. The
May report will also contain the results from the previous year and the November report will
contain the proposed POA for the upcoming year. These reports will contain detailed
information on the progress in the implementation of the physical, financial and impact
indicators.
16.   The semi-annual reports should contain the following information:
A. General Status
1) Major recent developments
2) Adherence to and deviation from POA
3) Problems encountered and solutions adopted
4) Benefits realized to date



4                                Annex H: Project Supervision, Monitoring and Evaluation
B. Project Administration
1) Fund flows and Government budgeting
2) Disbursements
3) Procurement
4) Financial reporting (accounts and audits)
C. Organization and Management
1) Project coordination and management (SGP coordination staff, Executive
Committee, Regional Offices, Technical Areas)
2) Monitoring and evaluation
